Daniel Teal

Attached Dispenser Closeup 2 11.2.14.png.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
11/2/2014 11:16

This is a close-up picture of the bottom of the ball dispensing mechanism. The tube from the blower and the bottom of the robot connects on the left, then the balls round the u-joint and come back through the right.

  • The servo can move the “hook” clockwise anywhere from in the middle of the tube, completely blocking it, to the little space besides the tube where the servo is, which leaves the tube open.

  • Before the match, we can close the hook, balance a small ball in the crook of the hook, and put a large ball on top. When the hook partially opens, the small ball can fall through but the large one can’t. When the hook is fully open, the large ball can fall through.

  • During the match, we can store balls simply by moving the hook to the middle of the tubes to stop balls from falling through.

Ismael Flores

@ismaelflores
10/30/2014 11:17

@gerrycocco I’m not Daniel (obviously), but from what I understood looking at the real life assembly yesterday and speaking with Daniel, there were no concrete plans to close off the sides (electronically, it might make replacing batteries and such a bit tedious). However, now that I think about it, maybe some laser-cut acrylic panels would look nice and more easily applied blower-side (opposite of shown in perspective shot). We can only really reduce the openness electronics-side simply because they are there (slots to reach in and pull out? Because, the large wiffle balls have a diameter greater than the thickness of either the battery or NXT).

Otherwise, if the balls do get stuck (very likely no more than one on either side, /if/ that), then we would be “controlling” them, for they are stationary with respect to the robot and not counted “dead” (Post 80 http://ftcforum.usfirst.org/showthread.php?3035-Game-Rules-and-Game-Play-Answer-Thread/page8). However, that could potentially not be an issue depending on how the driver reacts (easily resolved with care, in my opinion).

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
12/30/2014 14:04

Are you printing the pieces for the new u-turn?

Do you have clear tube for the short section in the head?

What is the purpose of the opening latch in the right side of the u-turn? Is it for loading balls?

Daniel Teal

@dteal
12/30/2014 20:09

I am going to print the pieces. I haven’t started yet but don’t foresee any trouble.

We don’t have the clear tube yet. Marek found some from U-Line (http://www.uline.com/Product/Detail/S-8110/Tubes/3-x-24-Clear-Plastic-Tubes). It’d be great if we could get a 12” sample piece - do you think you could manage that? If not, it wouldn’t be hard to build something similar out of thin plastic sheet.

The latch deals with air pressure. This new dispenser is completely airtight so that balls are shot down into goals (much faster than falling), which should help accuracy. However, if the dispenser remained airtight while storing balls for endgame, we’d block the airflow and more balls can’t come up. Thus the valve exists. For ease of use, the servo controlling the valve uses the same range of motion as and is linked to the servo that controls the ball retaining latch (now improved, by the way) such that we can control both with the old setup and a Y-cable. When the latch is open, the valve is closed, and vice-versa.

To load balls one still has to put them in through the bottom. I couldn’t figure out a simple, robust way to load them in the top. That said, the part hasn’t been printed yet, so I may be able to think of something.

The other big addition is a worm gearbox in the front (barely visible in the picture) which can finely tune the length of the string used to hold down the lift. It should make things much simpler.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
1/26/2015 21:07

1) What does “added timing” mean? 2) To verify: No additional testing was done for the “gyro dance” problem. Explicit scenario testing that is 3) We still have no “bump” sensor or even a hard “bump” mechanism? 4) In spite of all that, we can “easily” score over 500 pts on our own?

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
1/26/2015 21:32

@gerrycocco 1) As in we no longer rely only on encoders (which would stall if pressed up against the side of the structure) in the final “move up to center goal and drop” phase. Instead, I shortened the distance on the encoders a bit and added a forward for ~1 second after that so that we can use the bumper without stalling due to the encoder counts not increasing. 2) Yes, but we also have not seen the issue once in practice for weeks (in other words, since I removed priorities from the tasks). 3) No sensor, the addition of the timing component in 1) was implemented for a “hard bump” mechanism but the current latch is not rigid enough when extended to work (instead of acting as a bumper, pushing it up against the side of the wall causes it to bend inwards, allowing the robot to move closer than it should). 4) Yes, we were 100% successful in auto today and scored over 500 points every round, the highest being 555 I believe (correct me if i’m wrong @kylahayworth @blakehance1 )

Battery Voltage Data

Battery Voltage and RPM data graphed over the course of one match for all of the new batteries. Collected at Vandegrift scrimmage 4/14/15.

Autonomous

How do we knock over the kickstand and/or deposit autonomous balls in goals?

Checklist

Test Gyro Glitch - does it occur on our field?
Patch Gyro Glitch - sounds like a single thread for all sensors may be the potential fix
Tweak Center Goal - just to make sure it still works
Move from Center Goal to 90cm - consistently
Move from Ramp to Field - still works?

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
9/30/2014 20:45

So we might have a work around for all the NXT problems are encountering trying to read-in sensors, such as encoders. As posted below, we have 50mA of 5v power to work with. According to this thread: http://forum.arduino.cc/index.php/topic,5536.0.html a small Arduino (such as this one https://www.sparkfun.com/products/11113) uses pretty much no more than 35mA when running code. If this the case, we should be able to use an Arduino to read in any encoders/other sensors, keep track of the displacement, and then send the data to the NXT over I2C or pretty much any other communication protocol. This would ensure we don’t loose any steps in the value, and free up the NXT from the CPU intensive process of reading in those sensors.

That’s pretty much my only idea when it comes to tracking displacement. We don’t have enough power for a optical mouse, as it so turns out.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
10/21/2014 12:22

We have (at least) two open issues on autonomous IR following:

1) We need to “smooth” the input from the sensor if we want any consistency. See discussion: http://stackoverflow.com/questions/3760506/smoothing-values-over-time-moving-average-or-something-better Especially entry #3 (picture above). There is also a simple “smoothing” algorithm in here: http://opensourcehardwaregroup.com/tutorial-23-smoothing-data-old-version/ which implements a simple rolling average.

We probably need a combination of smoothing AND glitch detection. I think we occasionally get zeros, which will really mess up autonomous. We need to be able to distinguish a miscellaneous zero and the “drop to zero” when we go out of range.

2) We need to decide if we want to use Raw sensor data or composite “direction” data (or a combination of both).

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
10/21/2014 12:45

I think another thing we need to be careful with is ONLY reading IR for a short known period of time where we should get good results. That is, only when we think we are in front of an IR that we are aligning to. when we are turning or moving to “position 2” to check, we aren’t interpreting IR data.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
9/14/2014 11:06

The idea of the perfect robot that’s floating around in everybody’s mind goes something like this:

  • Scores balls (one in high goal), displaces kickstand, etc. in autonomous
  • During teleop, we head straight for the 90cm goal. It attaches to the robot so that we can fill it up while driving. When full, it’s placed onto the ramp and we head for the next goal. Repeat until endgame, when we fill up the center goal.
  • During endgame (and after the center goal is full), as many alliance members as possible should crowd onto the ramp (which already holds all three filled goals).

Like we thought: “A: The Center Goal scoring starts from the bottom of the Center Goal, not from the floor. The center Goal is 30 cm tall so the maximum score for filling the Center goal is 30 cm X 6 pts/cm = 180 pts.” This is response 22 on the FTC Game Q&A.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
9/21/2014 18:58

Similarly, answer 34 (see http://ftcforum.usfirst.org/showthread.php?3035-Game-Rules-and-Game-Play-Answer-Thread/page4) confirms that goal heights are measured from the bottom of the goal and not the floor. The scoring is as we had assumed; there is no infinite scoring potential.

says “Robots may not tip over ANY Rolling Goal (deliberately or accidentally)” and the game manual defines tipping over as more than 90 degrees so the balls would roll out of the tube. Can a robot deliberately tip a rolling goal less than 90 degrees to assist in scoring? For example, could a robot tip a rolling goal down to allow its alliance partner to more easily insert balls into the lowered end of the ball tube? Or tip a rolling goal onto its alliance’s ramp and then load balls into the tube from the ramp if the tip is less than 90 degrees?

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/6/2014 19:57

Regarding large vs. small ball pickup: it may be better to go for broke first.

Thought process: Assume that we have a robot that can only pick up large balls and are matched up against one other robot with the same speed. (1) if the other robot can only pick up large balls, it’s a tie. (2) if the other robot picks up large and small balls (at the same speed per ball), we fill up more tubes in a given time - until we run out. (3) if the other robot picks up only small balls (at the same speed), we win.

This looked like a promising idea, so we ran a small simulation, wherein two robots could pick up ~1 large ball per second. The large balls were exhausted about 1:15 into the match. All 90cm and 60cm tubes were full. I assume that if we do the same thing but with scenario (2) then we’d do even better with a large-ball-only robot.

This also raises the possibility of a robot that picks up all the large balls, then switches to small ball pickup. It could be done, but is more complex and prone to failure. However, if four equally competent large-ball-only robots are on the field, the ball supply may be depleted in only 30s (or 1 minute, realistically). Which leaves us half the match and a bunch of small balls.

This also leaves out endgame. Do we want to store four large balls one minute in? Do we ask our partner to herd them into a corner and wait for us?

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/6/2014 21:20

It’s sounding to me like a Large followed by Small strategy is the way to go. It should be easier to make than a system that can do them at the same time, appears to be faster scoring overall than the other ideas, and we can always take away a portion, rather than adding one. Plusssss, it’s actually more robust, since the two systems are pretty much independent. if one tube where to tear open, we would be able to switch to the other and not be out for the match.

As for endgame, I say we really are going to have to play that by ear as the competition goes on. It doesn’t have a major bearing on our decision here unless we decided on a small-ball-only strategy, which I doubt we are.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/7/2014 10:37

As far as endgame goes, I think we need 30 small balls to fill up the center goal.

  • If we can get one ball per second, we take all of endgame and have no time to park.
  • If there are two large balls in the goal from autonomous, I’ll assume we need 15 small balls. Then we take about 15 seconds and have some time to park afterwards, assuming no problems.
  • If we use large balls, we can fill up the goal in 5 seconds (or immediately, because we only need to position ourselves by the goal beforehand).
  • If there are two large balls in the goal from autonomous, we only need to store three. It may be possible to store those three and continue to feed small balls slowly.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/7/2014 10:38

We could both store balls and switch between modes as long as we have some way to store them at the top.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/7/2014 10:59

I just remembered we don’t get two large balls in autonomous; one is small. The same strategy should still apply.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/7/2014 18:47

We’ve decided to go with an all-large-ball strategy. The small balls simply aren’t worth the effort. Furthermore, we’ll store large balls for the endgame (likely, we’ll have a queue of balls: four are stored in the robot. When one more pops in, one more can go out into a tube. That way we can still score in other goals while waiting for endgame).

Also, we thought it might be helpful to carry two rolling goals along with us. We fill up one and don’t have to traverse the field to get another. The simple way to do this is to place the back caster wheel of the one we’re already holding over the rim of another’s base - a train of goals! This will only work if we can lift goals straight up, which we’re planning on doing anyway to get them on the ramp.

Finally, looking back at the rules, it is still perfectly legal to use a fold-out platform for another robot to drive on (unless there’s a ruling against it in the Q&A, but the manual heavily implies that it’s okay).

@dteal
9/18/2014 19:37

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
9/19/2014 10:52

I bet that would work better than @marektravnikar ‘s cardboard mockup :-).

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
9/30/2014 22:07

After a bunch of testing today (and I do mean a bunch), it looks like we are not going to get the Venturi effect we are looking for. While we can indeed establish a decent vacuum, able to pull in large balls, once we add a tube and have a ball moving up it we loose the Venturi Effect and in fact we then start loosing pressure out the intake (pressure that the gearbox is working very hard to make). It is my belief that we will not be able to establish a sole vacuum intake for the robot.

That being said, all hope is not lost! This system easily works with a force feed mechanism, such a revolving door concept, while maintaining pressure. While more complex and more work than the vacuum system, it should produce identical results in terms of ball cycle time. Additional experimentation is needed (and will probably happen tomorrow).

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
10/1/2014 12:15

I assume this is still with Tetrix motors. Also, how are we doing on golf balls?

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/1/2014 16:17

This is still, indeed, with Tetrix motors. As for golf balls, I constructed and tried my tube idea, but it didn’t work out. We have pretty much come to the conclusion that we can’t get the golf balls up the large tube. However, if we are worried about running out of large balls, then we had the idea to have a secondary small tube that runs along the large tube, sized for golf balls. That way, when we reach the point in the match where the large balls have all been scored, we simply flip a valve and divert the small balls and air to the small tube, which at that point would work even faster than the large tube would for the big balls.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/6/2014 20:05

As far as ball sorting goes, if we want to take in only large balls as fast as possible, then we need to cull out the small ones faster than that. We could feasibly mount some flat plate about 1.8” from the ground. A zip tie harvester in front would push small balls underneath, and the large balls would roll on top.

I tried something like this on the field: it works, but the large balls need a significant upwards force to make the jump; the ends of the zip ties are too flexible at 6” or so to push them up. It still might work if the center of rotation of the zip tie harvester is closer (~4”) to the ground and carefully positioned in front of the plate.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/6/2014 21:08

I like this idea. Perhaps we could could make the plate actuated, thus allowing our large ball only mode, and then when out, our small ball only mode by lowing the plate closer to the ground. Also, with thick enough zipties, or zipties that are weighted at the end (perhaps with a small nut or washer) we could probably get this to feed quite well over the 1.8” step.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/7/2014 18:49

If we’re going with large balls only, it’s even easier. Our earlier experiment showed that a plate and zip ties alone aren’t sufficient; we need a grippy rolling bar on the edge of the plate. The zip ties force the balls against the bar, which then rolls them up. We can then have multiple zip tie combines afterwards to raise the balls higher in the robot - all the way to the air pipe. Then we can mount the blower on the bottom of the robot, directed up, and avoid the need for 90-degree bends in the airflow.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/8/2014 21:06

I think two tests are in order!

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/9/2014 06:20

Tests are definitely a go.

The reason the roller bar / zip tie combo works is not that the balls are lifted by the zip ties. Rather, the zip ties press the balls against the roller bar, where friction takes over and moves them to the top. So, while surgical tubing may be sticky, it needs stiffness to sandwich the roller and ball hard; the greater the force, the greater the friction and thus reliability. The tubing may or may not be stiff enough; I don’t know.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
10/9/2014 11:05

Which was most effective do far? The “paddle wheel”? Maybe a V shaped one to center the balls?

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/9/2014 11:26

The paddle wheel worked because it was stiffer than short zip ties. I think something like it will work best in the end, but the paddles will need to have some flexibility or else it will be much easier to jam.

A “V” shape could work… we should try it.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
10/13/2014 12:12

We have some 3 inch tubing on order. 2 mil and 4 mil. 25 feet of each. It will ship shortly.

EDIT: 10/13/14

We got the sample into today and they measure it differently than we do. What we thought was a 3 inch diameter, is the actually 1/2 the circumference. They measure their tubing “flat”. We actually need something around 7” using their measurement technique. That would be the closest (without being too small).

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/13/2014 16:36

Where did you find the tubing? If you’re just asking bulk suppliers, the place I found earlier in the year should work: http://www.discountplasticbags.com/products/4.5%22-x-2150%27-x-2-Mil-Clear-Poly-Tubing-%28ET1%252d04520%29.html.

The correct “flat width” we need is 4.5” (5” is way too big). Problem is, that’s a 2.86” diameter, which is only about 1.5mm larger than the ideal large ball diameter. If we’re lucky, there will be an amazing seal and the ball will fly up better than before. If not, the ball will get stuck.

Because the theoretical fit is so close, thinner tubing (<2mil) may be better than thicker tubing because it’ll stretch and bulge out more easily to accommodate the ball.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/13/2014 16:44

Intake Roller: I cut up that foam and wrapped it around a wooden dowel, holding it in a spiral with springs (which are amazing; I wish we could buy fifty 7”x0.25” and use those instead!) and zip ties. The end result looks like the Roomba roller but Wiffle-ball sized. It’s very pliable and seems to work for baseballs (i.e., takes them in and moves them to the center).

For future reference, the lateral “Archimedes screw” action works when the thread is at least tilted 45 degrees from the axis of rotation (think double helix: DNA!). And what if we could 3D print this? Ninjaflex plastic, which I’ve never tried, may work… see (http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZnJZTwrQcQI).

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/31/2014 23:37

As I watch the shape of the gearbox come together, I am realizing how difficult it will be to lubricate this thing well. The only good solution I can think of right now would be to dry lubricate all of it, via a wax lubricant such as Boeshield t9, which is some really good stuff that we happen to have. While harder to apply and maintain, it will be so much cleaner and more efficient than any grease or oil. This stuff is also highly recommended for chains, so I think we should use it on the wheels too. Might as well keep the whole robot dry and clean.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
11/2/2014 17:32

We need to order the bushings for the gearbox. As demonstrated with the previous prototype gearbox, the standard Tetrix bushings do work, but not for long (especially on the high-force side of the gearbox). These bushings here should be used for the first and possibly second stages (http://www.mcmaster.com/#7815k59/=ufbyz8) (not cheap) and these others should be used for all stages down the line (http://www.mcmaster.com/#6338k311/=ufbzgx). We also should order hardened 3/16 shaft stock and avoid the Tetrix D profile shaft like the plague, for it also contributes significantly to wear (http://www.mcmaster.com/#1327k53/=ufc1i8) (<- example 12” section)

Also, so that we have it around and can make a spare, we should order a 5mm shaft incase the one we have is or becomes bent (http://www.mcmaster.com/#1265k44/=ufc2c0).

Lastly, the T9 wax lubricant works best when applied to very clean surfaces and allowed to cure for a day. So we should probably do this monday (I think we are short one large gear, so for time’s sake we may want to barrow one from old 4290 and return it as soon as we get another in).

Ball Manipulation

Game elements launched by Robots should not have a velocity greater than that required to reach a maximum of five (5) feet (1.5 meters) above the playing field surface, nor travel a horizontal distance greater than ten (10) feet (3 meters) from the point that the game element ends contact with the Robot. Parts of the Robot itself may not be launched.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
9/16/2014 20:24

We used a strobe light Monday to find that the rotational speed of the stock leafblower is some multiple of 1500 rpm. Today, Marek’s laser tachometer puts it at 13,500 rpm.

Accordingly, we added (yet more!) gears to our test platform to achieve a ratio of 23332 (or 1:108). Three attached Tetrix DC motors achieved a free spin rate of 15000 rpm (a bit lower than the theoretical 16,200 rpm, as expected - friction). A computer fan blade attached to the output shaft makes a significant breeze and lowers the rate to 6000 rpm, but we’re getting there!

Next up is to modify and attach the original leaf blower impeller to the prototype geartrain.

Daniel Teal

@dteal

@dteal
9/16/2014 20:28

The current thought is this: we begin with a thin plastic tube crumpled to fit in 18”. When the match starts, a spring loaded lift raises the tube to its full height (4’ above the floor). Air-propelled wiffle balls can enter the tube, rise to a constant four feet, then drop into whichever goal is currently behind the robot (some ball guide there may be in order).

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
9/16/2014 22:38

A thought on what rotor system we will use the actual air, the more I think about it, the more I am sure we need something what relies on centrifugal principles to move the air and build pressure (short of an actual air compressor). This is what is seen in leaf blowers, shopvacs, turbo chargers, etc, not the traditional angled blades or air foils which are designed for non static conditions such as CPU fans, plane props, ceiling fans, etc.

Also notice how the first group are all very high speed rotors, (aka blowers, according to the ASME) where as the latter are all very low speed (aka fans according to the ASME). This is a trend that I am willing to bet we will need to follow, and probably why our “Tetrix” CPU fan doesn’t work all that well for this application.

As well, we were considering a squirrel cage for, but following the trend above, squirrel cages are typically “forward-blade” and thus not super at static pressure. While it is still a “centrifugal-fan” (http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrifugal_fan) the differences (based on blade direction) pretty much dictate forward-blade -> high-flow/low-pressure vs backward-blade -> low(er)-flow/high(er)-pressure. Squirrel Cages out of cars, microwaves, and similar will all have forward-blades

Thus, when looking for alternatives to our leaf blower, we should look for forward bladed, or even straight bladed configurations if we want to get the pressure we are looking for.

We don’t want to be fans of it, but rather for it to really blow.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
9/17/2014 07:12

nice analysis! fluid dynamics is outside of my skill set, not the math, just how it actually works! It looks like we now know what we need to concentrate on and what not to. This is all part of prototyping and why you have to do it!

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
9/17/2014 21:37

According to this basic graph I found based on the Fan Affinity Laws, RPM, pressure, and power are indeed not directly related. By doubling the RPM, we double the flow, quadruple the pressure, but octuple the power needed.

Thus, this means currently, using a system of gears and bushings like we have now, (which is probably VERY inefficient at high RPM) we would need 32 (our current 4 to get 7000 RPM times 8) tetrix motors to reach identical or slightly better performance than the leaf blower.

This is further backed up by the chart of the motor from the leaf blower (RS755wc-6522) that can be found here http://www.mabuchi-motor.co.jp/cgi-bin/catalog/e_catalog.cgi?CAT_ID=rs_755vcwc . The leaf blower runs the motor nearly at the maximum efficiency, so they give us the output power there at 137W. So basically we MUST have that much out put power to reach the same performance with that blower. Currently, Tetrix motors put out 10.5W at their maximum power(http://www.patronumbots.com/uploads/1/3/1/6/13161577/tetrix_dc_motor_specs.pdf), and thus we can get a total of 40W out of those 4 motors, not accounting for the gear train.

Speaking of the gear train, we can calculate the amount of power we are actually loosing through them. Since right now we are running half the speed of the leaf blower motor when it is running at 137W, that means we really are getting 1/8th of that at the last shaft, or only 17W!!! That means currently it has less than a 50% efficiency!!

Ok so lets see if we can cheer this monologue up some, with facts! Turns out the Andymark motors actually put out 15W (measured, not theoretical: http://www.andymark.com/NeveRest-Motor-p/am-2964.htm) at their maximum power, and thus 4 of them would give us 60w to play with, then assuming we can get the gear train up to a 80% efficiency (I think that is a pretty reasonable number, we then have 48W at the end shaft, which is 2.8 times more than with our current system. thus 2.8^2/3 = 2, or the increase in pressure factor after these fixes. Thus I predict (if we can achieve the above) we will have 9800 RPM, and twice the pressure we have currently. This is 75% the speed, 57% the pressure, 43% the power of the current leaf blower as a leaf blower. So basically that 57% is the important one. Is it more than we have now, heck yeah. Is it enough, I don’t know.

So in the end, all this math leads to is just a definite maybe.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
9/18/2014 17:15

The plan, I think, is to use a variable number of motors (at least 2) with 3D-printed gears to drive (1:2) a printed 72-tooth XL pulley / gear combo that runs on a dead 5/16” allthread axle. We’ll run that to another printed dual pulley with 10 and 72 teeth on a 5/16” dead axle (which can be moved to tension the belts). Finally, that will power a 10T acetal pulley (for a total ratio of 1:103.68) from McMaster that should fit on the .2” (~3/16”) shaft the impeller is currently mounted to. This shaft will be live and mounted in several 5mm flanged bearings. We’ll also need to drill out more of the Tetrix shaft collars to fit on it. Also, for reference, both belts are XL, 3/8” wide, and 19” in circumference.

We should be able to mill / laser cut two parallel plates that will be held together by 5/16” bolts. The impeller will be outside both plates, which should make it easy to fit on the housing. We’ll make certain of that once Marek builds the cardboard housing (or we cut out and use the old one). The final goal is to have enough air pressure to move the balls 4’ up the 3” plastic tube. And, if we build this well enough, we might be able to use it without modification on the scrimmage robot.

I’ll try to make all the CAD files for the pulleys / gears tomorrow and print things over the weekend. Marek, can you get to TechShop?

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
9/22/2014 21:07

One thing I just realized: the 5mm bearings we ordered might not be able to handle the thrust forces that the blower is supposed to apply. Typically bearings can handle up to 30% of the radial force rating (this one is rated at 97 lbs) in the axial direction, but that number goes down the smaller the bearing is. The force we will apply is only a couple pounds, but it’s going to be cutting it close. In the end it only means shorter bearing life, but come the next iteration of the gearbox, we probably want to change out those bearings with their slightly larger, 2.5 times as strong, $0.80 more counterparts, or add a separate thrust bearing.

@danielteal1, we also need a way to transfer this force to the inner race of the bearing.. Do we have any spacers that will fit on the 5mm shaft, yet not be to big as to touch outer race or shield? This will be a must by some means if we don’t have a thrust bearing.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
9/25/2014 23:27

I’m a little worried on the higher ratio, and if the motors will be able to turn it well. It is now ~1:128 rather than the 1:104 we had originally planned for. Based on previous calculations, our target RPM is ~9800 rpm for this new gearbox. With the 1:104 ratio we would have had the Tetrix motors right at their maximum power output speed of ~90 RPM, but with the new ratio they will be running lower at 74 RPM. Now the power curve at that point is gentle enough that we likely won’t loose much, however the motors will get hotter and draw additional (wasted) amps. The Andymark motors (supposedly) will take this fine, but we might damage the Tetrix motors.

However, my calculations below were also assuming 4 Andymark motors (kinda making the above a little pointless) which are equivalent to about 6 Tetrix motors. Thus I suspect (and guessing here, as I don’t want to do the math) we will only get about 8000 rpm out of the new gearbox with the current 4 motors. Only testing will tell.

Lastly (to hopefully cheer this card up), despite the negativity above, I am highly confident that the new gearbox will function better than the old one.

For documentation purposes: Today we further tested the new gearbox to disappointing results, with the new gears and tensioning, we were able to increase the RPM by bout 500 or so, it was still lower than the gears. This is somewhat significant because pressure increases with the square of the RPM, so lowering the RPM hurts us doubly so.

This being said, we still used the rig for confirmation of the ability to lift the large balls, and it did very well (until it self-destructed). This proves that we have a good margin of operation to push up the large balls, even when the large balls are pushing up others as well. When we move back to a gear based gearbox, improve the efficiency of that (bearings + lubrication), and eventually add AndyMark motors (ordered today) we should pass our magic 7000 RPM number from original testing, hopefully by a lot.

This being said, we still have the problem of small balls not being able to be pushed up the tube. This is a matter of airflow (not pressure like the large balls), and is because the tube for the large balls is, well, larger. With more airflow we could get it to work by brute force, but I am skeptical if we will reach that number. If we turn out not to be able to, all hope is not lost. I have a strong gut feeling that we can do some clever work with the tube alone to make it effectively lift the small balls alone (since they lift fine when followed by a large ball), but I currently don’t have a solid solution. This something that can be figured out later with little modification to anything else.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/3/2014 16:08

The original reason we rejected the continuous conveyor belt design was that we couldn’t figure out how to fit a 4’ high conveyor within an 18” robot. One way to do just that is to run the belt on only two idlers (one at the top, one at the bottom), and start the match with the conveyor packed into as small a space as it would fit. Then a spring-loaded lift would rise the conveyor to a constant 4’.

The rest of the design easily follows. Scoops on the conveyor will pick up balls at the bottom (which are delivered on a ramp (see prototype)), and the balls are deposited at the top (which involves catapulting them into a chute at high speed (see prototype)).

The advantage is that it requires less power than the blower and can be run as fast as we can turn a belt idler (probably ~1.5s to raise a ball to 4 feet). It’s also simple to store balls simply by stopping the conveyor. The disadvantage is that we’d get a higher center of gravity and there are many more moving parts that can fail than the blower.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/3/2014 16:16

@marektravnikar Like Marek said, our initial impressions of the AndyMark motors were promising. They are about the same weight as the Tetrix motors, have the same gearbox diameter (so the mounts are interchangeable), and also have holes on their face (in case we want a different way to mount them). They’re still really long (4” not including the shaft), but that shouldn’t be a problem.

The encoder is built into the back of the motor (and it’s all still the same price as the Tetrix motor that does not have an encoder) and it looks like we’ll need to buy separate cables if we want to use them. Still, because we won’t have to assemble them ourselves (and get them dirty) and they have lower resolution, my guess is that we’ll get better results than with Tetrix encoders.

The motors also come pre-wired with about 1’ of cable, which is nice: we won’t have to solder them.

Time will tell whether they hold up better than the Tetrix DC motors, but, again, they probably will.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
10/3/2014 16:44

comment below “they have lower resolutionI“ encoders, do you actually mean more or higher* resolution?

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/3/2014 17:03

I mean lower resolution, which is probably a good thing. The Andymark Neverest motor’s encoder outputs 280 pulses per revolution (ppr). The Tetrix encoder gives (I think) 1440 ppr. So the Tetrix encoder is more accurate if we catch every pulse. I don’t know if this has or has not ever been a problem. With a lower resolution, though, we stand a much better chance of catching every encoder pulse in time so that we don’t lose its position. Dust and grime on the encoder wheel is also less likely to cause a false reading (plus the new motor’s encoder comes pre-assembled, preventing this).

In any case, 280 ppr geared 1:1 to a 4” OD wheel gives us a positioning accuracy of about 1mm. And we don’t have to pay $79.95 per encoder.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/3/2014 21:10

It’s also a Hall Effect encoder, which generally are more reliable than the optical ones, and easier to catch all the pulses from.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
10/6/2014 19:45

For posterity: today we tried using a passive flap to close the intake tube; it’s normally closed but hinges on the bottom when a ball is pressed in. It works extremely well but takes force to open because the airflow wants it closed.

Next, we tried a large and small tube concurrently. We don’t quite have enough air to reliably keep both open at once (in which case we’d just sort the wiffle balls then send them up as before). It does work if we pinch one tube or the other.

Following that, we built a tree structure: all wiffle balls are put into one 3” hole in the vertical pipe. As before, the large balls go up. But the smaller balls fall down, roll down two strings (because we can’t block the airflow with a solid ramp), and end up in the mouth of a smaller tube branching off at an angle. This almost worked: the small balls would go up if the intake was covered and the flow in the 3” tube restricted.

Then we thought about strategy. See the “Strategy” thread. Do we want to pick up both small and large balls concurrently?

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
11/3/2014 19:31

Today we assembled the lift to find that, in the current position, it was a few inches too far forward to be in line with the the goal tube. A few idea’s (asides from adjusting it back some) came up to solve this:

Daniel suggested that we build a chute to guide the balls from the Lift tube, to the top of the goal. Testing with a piece of paper this seemed pretty reliable.

My idea was to have the goal latch tilt the goal forward the few inches so that it was again lined up with the lift tube. This did not seem as reliable but wasn’t bad.

However neither of these will work. These solutions, while good for the large tube, will prevent us from scoring in the small or medium tubes, which is not what we want. In order to make it such that the balls can be scored in tubes of all heights, we must make the the lift tube in line vertically with the goal tube. When testing this we found it was not the most accurate, however I suspect this is due to all the excess space that exists around the ball when in the lift tube. I think we should construct an insert that will direct the ball more straight downward, just like how a longer barrel on a gun makes the bullet travel straighter. Otherwise, we might have to resort to the rainbow spring (aka slinky).

Daniel Teal

@dteal
11/3/2014 20:37

I wouldn’t say that neither would work until we try them. However, the simplest solution is likely to tilt the lift more to bring it in line with the tube and add more afterwards. And let’s get the blower working before we worry about the output too much; the air may affect things some.

Electronics Setup for future reference: Port 1: Motor controllers (4 daisy, power shared) a. 1.Right Front Motor 2.Right Back Motor b. 1.Left Front Motor 2.Right Front Motor c. 1.Intake 2.Blower A d. 1.Blower B 2.Blower C Port 2: Servo controllers (2 daisy, power shared) a. 1.Nothing 2.Storage Latch 3.Tube Winch 4.Kickstand 5.Touch Sensor 6.Goal Latch Port 3: Mux Mux 1: IR Sensor Mux 2: Bump Sensor 1 Mux 3: Bump Sensor 2 Port 4: Gyroscope Sensor

Material for 4290 Chassis

  • 4290 - the “non-Freshman” as it will be known until we have a better name
  • we discussed material options for the 2014-15 4290 FTC Chassis a) Wood - would be very unique, comparable weight to aluminum b) T-Slot (probably 20mm) - worried about connectors: cost and strength c) Normal Aluminum Stock - harder to work with, helps with FRC skills

Checklist

Decision for material of 4290 competition robot

Daniel Teal

@dteal
6/28/2014 19:23

Reference the “No-step” mecanum test bot for wooden construction.

I thought wood worked very well: it was quick, cheap, and very strong. Hopefully it also reduces static problems. The only downsides that I can see are that complex, precise cuts (which can be made by our CNC mill) are required and I suspect the wood may act as a soundboard, increasing noise from the motors.

Because wood (plywood or even nice hardwood [even oak would likely be cheaper than Tetrix!]) often comes in sheets and we have a CNC mill, I’m thinking of layering plywood with cutouts for wheels, etc. and fastening it together with threaded rod (which allows for disassembly): see http://www.chiefdelphi.com/forums/showthread.php?postid=953499. Glue, on the other hand, may be stronger.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
6/28/2014 19:35

I’ve been talking to Marek (archived at notebook4290@gmail.com), and, apparently, TechShop has a laser cutter with a 17”x26” build envelope that can cut 3/4” plywood! Using it should help us quite a bit - it’s fast, needs no post-processing (like deburring CNC milled parts), has negligible kerf, and is insanely accurate. Slot-and-tab joinery (as in the picture below) is now easily within our grasp.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
7/30/2014 11:59

Calvin was a TechShop member. Anyone can come with me as a non-member, but it’s $25/day/person. That is opposed to the $50/month if you add on as a family member, which I bet if we ask nicely we can work a deal like that out for the team. As for laying out the parts, that is done in Adobe Illustrator/Corel Draw, so anyone can do that, then all one has to do is pop it on the cutter and away it goes. As far as accuracy, I have a example box I cut out on the laser cutter which I know I could not make with anything that was not a CNC machine (short of spending a few days on it, and at that point I would just weld myself one) but on the laser cutter it took about 15 min to cad, 10 min to prep the file, and 5 min to cut.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
7/30/2014 12:48

So besides you Marek, we don’t have anyone in TechShop. We will have to look into this more as the year progresses. Perhaps we will get a new recruit to the team that is a member or wants to join.

As for the speed of the build with a laser, that is impressive, but we have to look at what it is we want to build…and how we want to do it. Don’t forget we have Bert who is king of glue-ed up laminated pieces and parts… Sometimes these “old school” techniques are more impressive than a CAD and a laser cut part (which can be done by anyone - with CAD software and a laser cutter). The “old school” techniques take longer, but are much more impressive in the end. I look at it like the difference between a Textrix FTC bot and a “FRC style” FTC bot. The former is clearly faster to build, but not nearly as impressive as the latter.

Also, you can always design a robot that needs a laser cut part, but you could also design a bot that is “as good” that doesn’t need laser cut parts.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
10/22/2014 22:54

For reference: We should be able to get “true” 1/4” plywood at HomeDepot and cut it to the maximum size that can be held in the laser. The laser’s max cutting area is 28” by 17” (though we can stretch this a smidge, and it can hold slightly larger material too). The 1/4” clear acrylic I have is 1 13” by 23” piece and one piece like that with a blower shaped hole in it on one side. The gobs of frosted acrylic I have (p95, so 95% translucent) is 9.75” by some distance longer than the laser cutter. That being said, 1/4” acrylic is not that expensive at all, and I’d be happy to head down to the plastic shop and get whatever size (and color (no purple, sadly)) we need.

This was just to clear up the random numbers I was saying off the top of my head about what could be cut and what we have to cut.

R1D2 Prototype is ON! - Sep 6-7

UPDATE: 9/02/2014: Schedule for Sat 9/6

  • The plan is for everyone to attend 9-1.
  • PHreshman are expected to go to the 1-3 sessions (and let us know how it went).
  • The R1D2 team will leave at 1p and start design work. All other “Stalemen” are free to leave at that time.

9a-11 Everyone for Team Building exercises 11 Game Unveil 11:30 Lunch (free Pizza) 12-1 Mixed team strategy sessions 1-3 Rookie team workshops (our PHreshmen to attend)

UPDATE: 8/29/2014: The Test Pilot Team has decided it’s a GO! Here’s a summary of what we are doing!

Goal 1: Have a tele-operable prototype FTC robot with a functional manipulator to show off @ lunch on Monday 9/8. (No autonomous)

Goal 2: Get FTC field setup in the shop ASAP. At a minimum, have the boarders up and the foam tiles in to drive on.

Goal 3: On Monday - Form a “field element” team to build and populate the FTC field in the shop!

Goal 4: Jump start the D5 process or rather turn the D4 process completely on it’s head.

Definitions/Notes:

  • Drivetrain will be simple - use no-step, demo-bot or build a simple 2-wheel drive platform. Can’t decide this yet.
  • “Working Manipulator” - as we discussed, we want to “score everything somehow” if possible. We will do as much as we can in the time allowed.
  • Game Strategy/Design will be done all of Saturday afternoon at Gerry’s house.. No robot build on Saturday. This is thinking time.
  • Robot Build will start 8am at Marek’s in Dripping Springs (pending his parents OK). We should make sure we have most if not all of the necessary Textrix parts, motors, demobot, no-step, etc at his house Friday night. We may have to Bag and Tag it so he doesn’t tinker!

  • No Autonomous is planned.

  • We have contingency plans for including more interested parties in this for a parallel “virtual build”. This “physical” team is selected! The Test Pilots are Arthur, Ehsan, Daniel, Miles and Marek.

UPDATE: 8/29/2014: feasibility meeting in Bertucciland at lunch time.

UPDATE: 8/27/2014: R1D2 == 1 Robot in 2 Days! At the “recap” meeting, this topic came up and there is interest in exploring what it would take to pull this off. We have little planning time but will need to get to get and hash out many details before this is attempted.

In case you guys did not know, Robot in 3 Days is taking on FTC starting this competition, and starting Robot in 1 Weekend!

What is Robot in 3 Days you ask? Well they are a team of engineers that (starting in 2013) takes on the challenge of designing and building an entire FRC robot in 3 days. This past year there were actually 5 teams that took on the challenge. Now to emphasize this, these teams are made up of high level engineers that do stuff like this for living. The robots they build are often very buggy (3 days is very little time after all) but the concepts they come up with, and the proof of them they build is invaluable. You can find a design or concept of theirs on nearly every single FRC robot this year and it is the sole reason so many robot were actually able to play the game (unlike some years where you get a whole bunch of drivetrains and nothing else). While of course we need to design our own robot, we should definitely check out the stream they will have. At the very least it could be inspiring of something greater.

You can find out more information about this here: http://www.chiefdelphi.com/forums/showthread.php?threadid=130345

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
6/5/2014 07:22
Attached mecanumprogram.png.

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
6/5/2014 07:25

VEX forum had some neat diagrams on the programming of motors to get the desired directional force.

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
6/5/2014 16:20

I just found this post: http://wpilib.screenstepslive.com/s/3120/m/7912/l/171875-driving-a-robot-using-mecanum-drive. Even though it is for FRC, the code seems to indicate that this library https://github.com/rbmj/wpilib contains a already made mechanum_cartesian method which taking a gyro reading, x stick and y stick, properly executes the algorithm to drive the chasis (including field oriented control). Even if we dont use that libraries code, we should certainly take a look at it

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
6/5/2014 16:23

I traced the mechanum_cartesian method to here: https://github.com/rbmj/wpilib/blob/master/RobotDrive.cpp Seems it makes a call to RotateVector() (which just does some trig and returns the result) to compensate for gyro, then implements the math to drive wheels. This file should have all the math arthur needs to implement mecanum, all we need to do is port it for ftc.

Robot BUILD!

Checklist

Build extendable dispenser tube for increased accuracy.
Add infeed motor encoder for jam detection
Fix LEDs on right side of robot.
Put a second servo on the goal latch (conclusion: not required)
Shorten the goal latch to avoid tipping goals (conclusion: not required. If we tip goals, they tip in the direction we want them to, i.e., towards the robot in the only direction the tube extension will let them vary, thereby (probably) offsetting any tilt the goals have in the opposite direction. Plus, we're now only missing ~1% anyways)
Build more ball tubes (try to get printed mount ID > 2.95").
Fiddle with intake - make it better.
Practice, practice, practice.

Checklist

**Hardware Fixes**
- Rebuild ball latch
- Change out gears in blower
- Goal latch bump sensor
- Make lift raise an extra inch or so
- Prevent dispenser / goal latch collisions
- Try more polypropylene on intake
- Replace tube
- Make more ball tubes
**Possibilities**
- Preventative Maintenance: replace other three wheels

Checklist

**Hardware Must Do**
-- Auto: Center goal touch "bump tool" (attaches to goal lift/latch)
-- Make tube to fit lift height
-- Find tube for dispenser. Must be 3" ID, 11.5"+ in length.
-- Build steel counterweight to prevent rocking
-- Reinstall side panels.
-- Plug in servo wires. Will need to know correct port numbers.
-- Intake: Rebuild out of aluminum (or oak).
-- Intake: Try more polypropylene, etc. for speed
-- Drivetrain: Try speeding up NoStep (switching gears)
-- Add storage, goal indicator servos
-- Speed up drivetrain (switch sprockets to get 2:3 ratio)
**Hardware Tweaks**
-- Rebuild goal latch (or see "build goal lift" under "Harware Possibilities")
-- Fix intake motor mounting (it's loose)
-- Check all screws / add loctite / check wiring
-- Lift: Build worm gearbox for string (or other adjustment mechanism)
-- Make spare tubes

I also did a lot of work with the tube mounting pieces. I went head and made two sets of the two part clamping end plates (Bert’s idea I think), but it didn’t go so well. I went ahead and made some different end plates and allowed tube to just slide over the outside without having to flare it, and they appear to work really well. We can probably reuse them, but they use super glue as it seems to be best option for securing the ends. I also used these ends for the U-turn at the top.

These are also less tan 1/4 the mass of their steel equivalents (the ones we have). More specs can be found here http://www.technicalequipment.co.nz/ProductDetail.aspx?pid=409&cid=14 . CADs of these slides are also available.

But…. The big issue before was price. However, there is am ebay seller that is selling them new, inside the US, for $5.60 each (plus $9.60 shipping). While they are on sale right now, I really have no idea if this price is crazy low or not, since I can’t find the price normally. Regardless the seller has good ratings, and says they are new, so these should be fair slides (they are made for airplanes after all).

Thoughts?

I got the two slides in today, and I must say that these things are cool. They are indeed 15.75in closed length and have 18.11in travel, and are very tight (as in no slop) yet very smooth. What I was not expecting was how narrow (not wide) they are (15mm by 20 mm). This may also remove the need cross-bracing (though we made need it on the first stage since the bottom slides would still be steel). At 250 grams each, we will still be reducing the weight of our upper slides by over half, even when doubling up. As well, at $5.60, we would only need to invest about $30 in these (plus shipping and extras if want them).

Arthur Pachachura

@arthurpachachura1
12/19/2014 19:55

Here’s the PID code. Simple enough?

/**
* Provides PID-based rampup and rampdown
* @param power Target power, the PID setpoint
* @param measuredpower Immediate measured motor power
* @param dt Change in time since last measured reading
* @param error Last received error value... just keep passing the last value in
* @param integral Last integral value... just keep passing the last value in
*/
float ramp_pid(float power, float measuredpower, float dt, float& error, float& integral)
{
    float curerror = power - measuredpower;
    integral += curerror*dt;
    float derivative = (curerror - error)/dt;
    float output = curerror + integral + derivative;
    error = curerror;
    return output;
}

Daniel Teal

@dteal
12/20/2014 07:34

Simplest PID I’ve ever seen. Actually, I don’t know why we would use it for acceleration (just use something along the lines of Marek’s code), but it could be helpful for turning / slow movement.

Speaking of which, will the gyro lose fewer steps if we accelerate and decelerate turning?

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
12/20/2014 10:46

Speaking of gyro’s. At the FLL qualifier last weekend, one of the winning teams used the same gyro we did to navigate the field. We must be doing something wrong if it works for them and we are having so many issues. Maybe we should invite them in to consult :-).

Arthur Pachachura

@arthurpachachura1
12/20/2014 17:43

We know only three things about gyro accuracy:

  1. If you are moving the robot while the program starts, you’re in a hole. 2. It can only record x values per second. In other words, if your angular velocity is to high, values will be missed.
  2. If you jerk too quickly, gyro gives unpredictable results. In other words, the gyro should fail less if we set a cap on acceleration.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
12/20/2014 17:49

This is only true if it start working in the first place. I think the bigger problem is that currently we just turn in place during autonomous.

Arthur Pachachura

@arthurpachachura1
12/20/2014 18:53

@marektravnikar You’re right. We have a few ideas as to how to solve the problem and would be ready to run some test code when we get back to see if the problem was solved. We already (think we) know the issue: it’s a multi-threading problem.

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
12/20/2014 22:18

@marektravnikar Read: We basically rewrote the whole gyro code the weekend before finals, but can’t test it till the new year. It will probably break everything. Horribly.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
12/21/2014 19:55

@marektravnikar @gerrycocco About the goal lift… it can’t be built unless we make some major changes to the main ball lift. We can’t angle the main lift any more unless we use narrower slides, but I don’t want to use a single aluminum slide on every stage. Thus the ball tube has to be as close to the robot as it is right now so that the dispenser lines up, and the width of the goal base gives us about .5” of clearance on each side to build a lift. Besides, if we’re stretching the dispenser (getting rid of that surplus space when above the center goal), we have almost no vertical room in the center back, either.

The short of it is that I think we can build a much more reliable, simpler, and faster goal latch than goal lift; if we build a lift, it’s more likely to be like the first one and waste time rather than help us score twenty more points.

I think we’ll help to solve the ball-dropping alignment issue just by shooting balls down.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
1/5/2015 19:46

For reference, here is a complete list of the improvements we made over winter break:

  • Changed drivetrain to 1:1 (i.e., 1.5x faster)
  • Rebuilt intake roller (oak for strength and more polypropylene for speed)
  • Cleaned out the robot (dust)
  • Loctite on all bolts / nuts
  • Giraphphe etched on side panel
  • Rebuilt top “head” with…
  • Lighter drawer slides on top stage to help center of gravity
  • Worm gear adjustment of lift string
  • Dispenser is airtight and longer for more accuracy
  • Clear ball tube for easy visibility of stored balls
  • Height of dispenser = 48.5” (gets rid of extra space above center goal)
  • Ball latch rebuilt
  • U-turn contained within robot
  • Valve in dispenser top to permit airflow while storing balls for endgame
  • Valve servo hooked to ball latch servo to make use of existing wiring
  • Rebuilt lift has more vertical clearance to fit in 18”

Daniel Teal

@dteal
1/5/2015 19:58

Also, here is a list of everything that remains to be done:

Hardware / Electrical:

  • Find a better tube for the dispenser. Needs to be 3” ID, 11.5”+ length.
  • Build goal latch. Will include two touch sensors to bump the center goal.
  • Install sensor multiplexer for touch sensors.
  • Finish building a tube of the correct length for the new lift.
  • Add counterweight in front to minimize rocking from acceleration
  • Reinstall side panels.
  • Build flag / paint valve on dispenser for a ball latch indicator
  • Paint “Giraphphe” logo purple for visibility
  • Plug in servo wires (currently disconnected). Need correct port numbers.
  • Install LEDs

Programming:

  • Recalibrate the servos (they all have new positions)
  • Account for faster drive speed in autonomous
  • In autonomous, raise goal latch before raising lift to avoid damage
  • In autonomous, raise lift before driving (as the lift touches the floor now)
  • Remap controls for goal latch (no toggle) b/c there is no indicator servo

These are listed as checklist items either this card and the “Wiring/Program” card, respectively.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
1/20/2015 18:31

Update 1/20/15:

(1) The LEDs seem to draw too much current; battery voltage drops ~1v when they’re on. We plan instead to try them on a separate 9v battery which, while the LEDs will be dimmer, won’t affect our scoring ability.

(2) Marek handed me a plastic tube (was that the one you ordered, @gerrycocco?) and I mounted it on the dispenser at lunch. Works great, no problems whatsoever. The robot’s now completely functional again.

(3) The newly revised goal latch (v4.0) works, kind of. The servo now has barely enough torque to pull in a goal, but it is omnidirectional (goal can be in any orientation up to ~2” out from the robot). Also, when the robot rocks, the goal can come undone. We added friction tape and Blake tried accelerating while driving, the combination of which seemed to work. Still, we plan to add a second servo opposite from the first, which should solve both problems.

(4) In an attempt to solve the rocking issue, we put a heavy weight (5-10lbs) on the front top of the robot. The result: the robot rocked equally in both directions, rather than with a bias towards the back. The conclusion is that weight only helps at the bottom. We really need a lower COG. Unfortunately, there’s not much room at the bottom, but perhaps we’ll try to fit in some weight anyways.

(5) CONGRATULATIONS BLAKE, KYLA, AND EHSAN! We have officially scored over 500 points (503 total) in a practice match! The robot scored the center goal in autonomous, completely filled the high goal and center goal, and returned to the parking zone in the last second.

@arthurpachachura1 @ehsanmasdar You guys were looking for things to do (at least Ehsan was)! Figure out how to get a number (doesn’t matter if it is RPM or APW -Arthur’s Per Whatever). We may not want to have it on all the time. It may just be a testing thing. OR this might be something we dump to our log (once it is re-activated). I would think that if the log was a single file, contained only a few (up to say 50 or 100 lines) it shouldn’t affect our performance. If we put in that file just the basics: like gyro value at the “decision box”, time of ball dump, time of auto latch, time when blower turns on or off and a speed check every 5 or 10 seconds. We might want to note intake on/off - to correlate “stuck” balls. This “telemetry” data will also help for the “control award”.

At a minimum, we need to check blower speed. The idea is to confirm the system is functioning well, and that it may not be the blower..it might be the tube, etc. So can we do this? Don’t forget, we have a code freeze at the end of the week!

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
2/7/2015 14:03

@gerrycocco Teleop and auto each have a sort of “global clock” since enabled time. What I plan to do today is use that “global clock” and rewrite the logging lib to be a simple, one file library that logs an item with it’s position and an optional tag. So for example !!!START AUTO!!! 13s [IR] Decision was zone three with IR 0,32 !!!START TELEOP!!! 12s [Intake] Intake On 13s [Intake] Intake Off 14s [Blower] RPM 7000 19s [Blower] RPM 6500

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
2/7/2015 14:13

Great! Don’t make it to “wordy”. It can be shorthand since it is mainly for us.

10.0 BL 0 13.1 IR Z3 0,32 13.3 IN on 13.5 BL on 15.1 BL 5600

We would rather have more data points, with less words than the other way around. I suppose we could use 0/1 for on/off if we need to. But on/off is more readable and still short. I do think we need more granular than seconds (tenth’s seem ok). Operation should just be two or three characters. We shouldn’t need any more.

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
2/7/2015 15:05

@gerrycocco Everything is actually measured in millis, so I could get even more precise than tenths if needed. Anything else besides IR, Blower,Intake I should be logging?

Gerry Cocco

@gerrycocco
2/7/2015 15:52

Basically we only need to get down to a unit of measure that will show the order it happened and if it is a large (in human terms) distance apart. Milli-seconds is probably overkill, but not totally objectionable. I would recommend tenths or hundredths at the most for what we are logging.

As for what to log:

Definite: IR “decision box” info. Defiinte: Blower on/off and RPMs (or some unit we are calibrated to) Definite: Intake on/off - indicator of blockages or over feeds.

Maybe: Bump into tower; ball latch release Maybe: Goal latch - down/up Maybe: Kickstand servo - out/in Maybe: Override blower in last few seconds of match Maybe: I can’t think of anything else

The “Maybe’s” could be overkill. We really want to insure that the blower is functioning the way we want it to and the IR is turning at the right place. The rest of the stuff may come in handy for a “telemetry” discussion with the judges, but may not be more helpful than that.

Daniel Teal

Daniel Teal

@dteal
2/15/2015 19:01

Blower speed measurements, 2/15/15: Using the 2014 batteries, at 12.9v the wooden blower measured 7800 RPM while the acrylic one measured 8000 RPM. At 13.6v, the wooden blower measured 8200 RPM while the acrylic one measured 8300 RPM. Also, the battery voltage decreased quickly while at 13.6v: I could read the RPM counting steadily down (for which reason these measurements are accurate probably no more than +/-100 RPM), and, after thirty seconds or so, the battery voltage had decreased by ~0.4v.

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
2/26/2015 20:01

@danielteal1 @kylahayworth Let’s talk programming for this. By far the easiest (and safest) way to make this work would be to have the servo rotate forward while one button is pressed, and back while another is pressed. Only problem with this is that Kyla only has one button left on her controller. One thing we could do is remove the intake forward override, which was really a failsafe for if the auto unjam work, and then we would have two buttons free. The other option would be to have the servo be a toggle that rotates for a set amount of time. The con with this approach is that once you deploy or retract the servo, you won’t be able to control it until it is done retracting/extending (which, like daniel said, could be up to 10 seconds).

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
3/20/2015 15:44

@gerrycocco @danielteal1 @ehsanmasdar @arthurpachachura1 @kylahayworth @blakehance1 @coccojr Hey guys, so I was wondering where the idea stood on making the acrylic version of our robot. I kept putting it off saying “only if we make it to worlds,” but now that we have, we need to actually discuss it. Here is what has been discussed so far:

Originally we were discussing just making the bottom part (no lift or “head”) of the robot, but this time with 1/4” acrylic in place of the 1/4” plywood. This would act as home for the acrylic blower along with the spare set of mecanum wheels and the spare in feed, all together would give an excellent transparent display of what exactly is in our robot and how it all works together. It would just require the acrylic parts and nothing which we wouldn’t already have.

But then, while at the super regional, we got to thinking: Why not make the rest of the robot too? Since we would already have the blower and it’s 3D printed parts (perhaps the hardest part of the entire robot) and the mecanum wheels, if we just added some motors and electronics, we would also have not just a model of our robot, but a full blown, 100% compatible, incredibly good looking duplicate of it! The freshman were actually the ones who came up with idea, and offered the motors and electronics from 5998 to make it happen. Considering we have lots of extra drawer slides, motors, electronics, and NXT’s around, we don’t actually have to acquire that much outside of more acrylic and some 3D printed parts. But in the end, we would have a spare of every component on the robot, in addition to something to help us compete against Westlake for that PTC design award (we know it’s going to be one of us who wins it).

Anyways, that’s the summary of anything so far, but we need to discuss this. I, clearly, am in favor of it; Bert, to no surprise, is not (or at least he wasn’t as of a few months ago); and Cathy liked it as well (also as of a few months ago).

Kyla Hayworth

@kylahayworth
3/20/2015 20:31

@marektravnikar @danielteal1 @gerrycocco @ehsanmasdar @arthurpachachura1 @blakehance1 My opinion on this probably doesn’t matter too much, but I think that having an entire acrylic spare of our robot would be incredibly cool. From what both Ehsan and I can think of, here are the pros and cons: Pros: -Pit display that doesn’t have to leave for matches -Amazing display for judging -If inspectors really want to inspect our robot’s interior, they can look in the acrylic one -We’ll know where at least one set of spare parts is -Practice against ourselves! -Code team can tweak autonomous while drive team practices Cons: -Transport (?) -Cost -Would take time away from either you or Daniel-only a problem if there are big hardware changes that would take more than a day or so -Maintenance of second robot (?) -Take up a bit of space in the pit

SIDENOTE: If we do make an acrylic version, it should be named jellyPHish. Ha.

There are always pros and cons, so it is good to get all the options out there! We have to:

  2. Accommodate various methods of entry since EVERYONE on the team will be inputting to notebook. The “notebook editor” will really be the “voice” and keep things consistent. Daniel Teal

@dteal
11/2/2014 09:57

Wiring schematic:

  1. Port 1: Daisy-chained motor controllers

    • MC 1: Right front/back drive motors
    • MC 2: Left front/back drive motors (I think, for the scrimmage, we’ll give the driver control of both movement and rotation. Later, depending on the driver’s and operator’s preferences, we may let the operator rotate the robot).
    • MC 3: Intake/Blower A (The operator should be able to toggle the intake forwards / backwards / off).
    • MC 4: Blower B/Blower C (Note: Blower A, B, and C should ALWAYS be on at the same time and in the same direction, like the 4290 arm last year. Also, the operator should be able to toggle the blower on / off.)

  2. Port 2: Servo controller (there’s only one servo controller)

    • SC Servo 1: (NOT ON ROBOT) [Tube lift release servo (moves once at beginning of match during autonomous to release spring-loaded lift. Just in case, the operator should be able to move this servo, even though in practice he may never need to.)]
    • SC Servo 2: Output storage toggle servo (moves to store balls / retain them. If moved halfway, then fully, we can dispense the small ball first and a large one later in autonomous. I’ll demonstrate Monday. The operator should be able to control store / half-store / release.)
    • SC Servo 3: Goal retainer servo (latches onto goal to attach it to the robot. The operator should be able to toggle the latch on / off.)
    • SC Servo 4: (NOT ON ROBOT) Goal lift servo (quarter-scale 1260 degree servo that lifts the goal we’ve attached to. The operator should be able to move this up / down AND it may be helpful to have two locations to instantly move it to automatically: the right height to attach to a goal, and the right height to put the goal on a ramp.)
    • SC Servo 5: Kickstand hook servo (Can be used in autonomous. The operator should also be able to toggle in/out.)

3, Port 3: IR Sensor

  1. Port 4: Gyro

According to , teams may use the NXT sensor multiplexer (MUX), which comes with an external 9-volt battery box (also allowed).

Our team would like to use a MUX, but are concerned about adding another battery to the robot because we’ll need to make sure that it is charged before every match. As in post #51, of course, the MUX itself will not be modified.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
12/15/2014 15:43

The answer’s no:

A: No, you may only use the HiTechnic 9-volt Battery Box with the NXT Sensor Multiplexer as specified in c. A voltage converter would be considered additional robot electronics that is not specifically allowed by .

We’ll just make sure to put in a new battery before every competition.

Now that we know the LEDs draw too much current of the Tetrix battery, we hooked them up to a variable power supply for testing. At 12V they shine brightly and draw ~1.5A. At 9V they’re about half to two-thirds as bright but only draw ~0.25A. We figure we can use a 9V battery and a 9-12v DC converter (like https://www.pololu.com/product/2568).

Daniel Teal

@dteal
1/25/2015 09:53

Well, FIRST is somewhat hazy. They finally answered our question about using BlinkyLights (it’s a “no”), but their reason was that, (a) if we were to power the 5v BlinkyLights strip off the 12v main power, we’d need an “external power adapter,” which is not “an integral part of an allowed part” (although I don’t know why it doesn’t fall under the decoration exception), and (b) if we were to power it from a separate source, the “multiple batteries and built-in voltage regulator with a USB interface” we’d have to use don’t count as “no more than one (1) battery of any type not to exceed 9 volts”.

So it sounds like they don’t like voltage regulators or multiple batteries. Our best bet for something absolutely legal is a simple 9v battery. At 9v, the lights draw .25A, so we’d have to change it out every hour or so.

The good news is that I think FIRST has recognized how hard it is to do this. At the end of their answer, they state “the FTC Game Design Committee re-evaluates all of the robot rules annually, and although this rule won’t change in the current season, we will certainly discuss our rules around LEDs and power to LEDs for next year’s game.”

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
1/25/2015 10:40

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
2/7/2015 15:57

@marektravnikar @danielteal1 I would like to be able to do standard RPM on the blower. Given a clock with a precision of 1 millisecond and encoder counts, what would be the formula to calculate RPM for our blower, and what time interval should I measure to get a representative sample of the RPM?

Ehsan Asdar

@ehsanmasdar
2/7/2015 16:04

@gerrycocco @arthurpachachura1 Logging revamp done in https://github.com/lasarobotics/FTCLibs/commit/f53ef50dbd26a0a6484f3134efe233932ce68429. Just needs to be tested.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
2/7/2015 19:37

@ehsanmasdar Blower RPM = 4860000 x (current encoder count - previous encoder count) / (encoder counts per motor revolution x time elapsed in ms), where 4860000 = 81 x 1000 x 60 = gear ratio x ms/sec x sec/min.

You probably don’t need to measure any time interval smaller than 500ms, and one second might even do.

Marek Travnikar - Mentor

@marektravnikar
2/7/2015 19:41

@ehsanmasdar I agree with Daniel. In fact 1 ms would reduce our precision, and give us wild swings in rpm when it really doesn’t change at all. The bigger the time the better (to a point). @danielteal1 Did you account for the 7 pulses per rotation of the motor? I think it’s 7 (we should check) but it’s at least more than 1.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
2/8/2015 06:52

@marektravnikar @ehsanmasdar Although the encoder does measure 7 pulses per revolution (ppr), it measures the original motor position, not the 1:40 motor gearbox and output shaft position. So, for our purposes, the encoder measures 280 = 7 x 40 pulses per revolution of the motor output shaft (see http://www.andymark.com/NeveRest-Motor-p/am-2964.htm).

With that number in the formula, RPM = 17357 x (current encoder count - previous encoder count) / (time elapsed in ms), where 17357 = 81 x 1000 x 60 / 280 = gear ratio x ms/sec x sec/min / ppr.

If the RPM hovers around 8000 and we want a measuring error of less than one percent, the time delay must be at least 217 ms = 1 / (8000 x 0.01 / (17357 * 1)).

Daniel Teal

@dteal
2/8/2015 07:00

@ehsanmasdar Not sure how this works for you in RobotC, but (http://www.cougarrobot.com/index.php?option=com_content&view=article&id=331%3Aandymark-neverest-motor-notes&catid=92%3Aftc-hardware&Itemid=140) says that the number 280 is the cycles per revolution output by the motor, and, since quadrature encoding is used, it actually has a resolution of 1120 = 280 x 4 pulses per revolution. If that’s what you get in RobotC, than modify that “17357” to be 4339 = 81 x 1000 x 60 / (280 * 4).

2/11/2015 20:24

@gerrycocco I believe that this run was just a static blower test while Daniel was putting on the new tube, hence the lack of any intake on/off log entry (which is fully implemented). The battery is not fresh since this was not a full practice match, but either way the output of voltage seen during the blower running (under load) should be less than the battery measured when we start the program. How close the load voltage is vs the idle voltage determines how good the battery (some of the 2013- or worse batteries drop from 13 to 8 volts under load, while our newest 2014 battery drops from 14 to 12 volts). This, paired with the blower rpm (which is seems “about right” but has not been checked with the tachometer) should give us much more debugging into why the blower “seemed slow” in a match.

Daniel Teal

@dteal
4/15/2015 20:12
  • Over 30 square feet of 1/4” plywood
  • At any one time, the blower uses 200 watts of electricity to power 12 gears with a 1:81 ratio resulting in a speed of 8000 rpm at the leafblower impeller. This creates about 150 cubig feet per minute (CFM) of airflow to move balls.
  • Plastic parts printed on 4 different 3D printers over 36 hours of combined print time, using a kilogram of filament (400 meters / 1300 feet / quarter mile)
  • 6 hours of laser cutting (and the number of individual cuts is practically uncountable)
  • 300 LEDs on the robot
  • Total weight: 45 lbs.
  • Total height: 54”
  • Ball tube is made of 0.7 mil dropcloth and scotch tape
  • Can score over 600 points alone
  • Intake (made with 26 zip ties) spins at 300 rpm.
  • Total number of Tetrix c-channel parts used: 3
  • 24 man-hours of maintenance between each competition
  • Directions the holonomic drivetrain can move in: infinity
  • Matches won: 90%

The initial position of the rolling goals is described as in the center of the corresponding tiles. Since the field perimeter is slightly smaller than 12 feet, the starting tiles for the rolling goals are not entirely in the field area. Is the initial position in the center of the original, full tiles (and thus closer to the perimeter than the tile seam) or is the initial position in the center of the portion of the tile that is in the field (and thus half way between the perimeter and the seam, even though that is less than 1 foot from the seam)? Also, is there any particular initial rotation of the rolling goals (e.g., “pentagon point pointing towards the ramp”), or will they be in random starting rotations?

Answer: The initial position of the Rolling Goals is in the center of the tile. Official game fields will have the edges removed so that the tiles fit within the field borders. There is not any particular initial rotation of the goal other than the height being in their specific tiles.

@ehsanmasdar
9/28/2014 16:37

In QA answer #3, you state that fan blades and fans made out of those fan blades are legal. Along those lines, our team would like clarification on whether an entire fan, such as

http://www.robotshop.com/en/12v-16cf...lower-fan.html, or http://www.robotshop.com/en/sfe-air-pump.html

is allowed. In particular, would such a fan violate R04c prohibiting a COTS component with more than 1 degree of freedom? Or, would it violate RG10d prohibiting pneumatic devices? If a robot were to use mechanical switches to control or redirect the air current created by a fan, would the resulting system be considered a pneumatic device?

Thanks,

FTC team 8538 (LickRobotics)

Answer: Complete fan assemblies violate Rules R04c and R09 and are therefore not legal. Team built devices that blow air and do not result in appreciable increase in air pressure are legal providing no other rules are violated.
